| OBS-067-T2-063 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Psa 34:8, Psa 73:28, Est 5:9 | Goodness enables and deepens affect when genuine. Genuine encounter with divine goodness produces trust, praise, and gladness rooted in God — these are authentic affective states produced by the encounter. The comparative wisdom mode also has an affective dimension: the person who genuinely values "better than" outcomes experiences a right ordering of their desires and affections. Distorted goodness impairs affect: Haman's gladness-of-heart (tov-lev, Est 5:9) is genuine affect but immediately destroyed — it is affect without its proper root, which renders it constitutionally fragile. The diagnosis is that goodness properly produces deep, stable, rooted affective states; goodness-mimicry produces shallow, fragile affective states. (OBS-067-OBS-040, OBS-067-OBS-041, OBS-067-OBS-029) |
| OBS-067-T2-064 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Psa 73:28, Est 5:9 | The affective engagement pattern reveals that goodness is not a purely cognitive or moral-rational characteristic — it has a genuine affective root. The Haman diagnostic is the sharpest evidence: the inner-well-being state (tov-lev) is a real affective state, not a moral fiction. But its fragility reveals that affect without proper grounding in genuine goodness is structurally unstable. The stable positive affect of Psa 73:28 (the repositioned inner life of Asaph) contrasts with Haman's fragile gladness and reveals that genuine goodness produces affect that is resilient precisely because it is rooted in the enduring goodness of God, not in circumstance. Goodness is therefore affectively generative — it produces genuine, stable, and resilient positive inner states — but only when the goodness is genuine. (OBS-067-OBS-029, OBS-067-OBS-040, OBS-067-OBS-041) |
| OBS-067-T2-071 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Eze 36:31, Gen 1:31, Mic 6:8 | Moral evaluation is one of the most extensively engaged faculties. The not-good verdict mode (884-006, 22 verses) is itself a sustained moral evaluation activity — the prophetic and wisdom literature applies the standard of the good to conduct and names the verdict. The comparative wisdom mode (884-004) engages moral evaluation in its comparative form — ranking goods hierarchically. Eze 36:31 engages moral evaluation in the person's own self-assessment: the person comes to evaluate their own deeds as not-good. Gen 1:31 (884-007) is a divine moral evaluation — "God saw that it was good." Mic 6:8 presents goodness as the criterion against which conduct is evaluated. Moral evaluation is both engaged by goodness (goodness calibrates the moral evaluation faculty) and expressed through goodness (the good person applies the standard rightly). (OBS-067-OBS-012, OBS-067-OBS-035, OBS-067-OBS-036, SBF-067-Q065) |
| OBS-067-T2-072 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Mic 6:8, Mal 2:17, Pro 15:16 | Goodness enables and deepens moral evaluation when genuine; distorted goodness specifically impairs it. The enabling function: the standard of the good, as revealed and received (Mic 6:8 — "He has told you what is good"), calibrates the moral evaluation faculty. The person who possesses genuine goodness can accurately assess what is and is not good. The deepening function: the comparative wisdom mode actively trains the evaluative capacity — the better-than sayings develop discernment. The impairing function: Mal 2:17 / Jer 44:17 describe the condition where moral evaluation is inverted — the faculty is functioning but its standard has been corrupted. This is the most severe impairment: the evaluative faculty applied to a false standard produces confident but wrong moral verdicts. (OBS-067-OBS-012, OBS-067-OBS-021, SBF-067-Q065) |
| OBS-067-T2-073 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Mic 6:8, Mal 2:17, Eze 36:31 | The moral evaluation engagement reveals that goodness is inherently normative — it carries a standard against which everything is assessed. The programme's registry is named "goodness" but its data is full of moral verdicts, comparative rankings, and assessments. This confirms that goodness and the moral evaluation faculty are structurally co-dependent: genuine goodness calibrates moral evaluation (goodness is the standard); moral evaluation expresses goodness (the good person judges rightly). The corruption of goodness corrupts moral evaluation — the most severe distortion is not the absence of evaluation but its inversion. This reveals that goodness is not just a positive quality but a normative anchor for the entire moral evaluation architecture of the inner being. (OBS-067-OBS-021, OBS-067-OBS-027, OBS-067-OBS-049) |
| OBS-067-T2-074 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Eze 36:31, Rom 2:4 | Conscience is engaged most directly at Eze 36:31: "you will remember your evil ways, and your deeds that were not good, and you will loathe yourselves for your iniquities and your abominations." The self-loathing described is a conscience response — the inner witness of moral failure producing conviction. The sequence (remember → recognise as not-good → loathe) is a conscience sequence: the moral inner witness activates, names the failure, and produces the appropriate inner response (loathing/conviction). Rom 2:4's chrēstotēs "leading to repentance" also implies a conscience engagement — God's kindness acts on the inner being's conscience to produce the movement toward repentance. The goodness-conscience connection operates through the negative register: the recognition of not-goodness activates conscience; the encounter with divine goodness produces the conditions in which conscience can operate truthfully. (OBS-067-OBS-035, OBS-067-OBS-049, SBF-067-Q049) |
| OBS-067-T2-075 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Eze 36:31, Rom 2:4, Mal 2:17 | Goodness enables and deepens conscience — specifically through the not-goodness register. The recognition of not-goodness (884-006 and Eze 36:31) is a conscience operation enabled by the standard of the good: only a person who has access to the standard of genuine goodness can accurately recognise their own not-goodness and respond with appropriate conviction. The distortion form (Mal 2:17 / Jer 44:17) shows conscience impaired: when goodness is redefined, the conscience's witness becomes unreliable — it applies the wrong standard and produces false moral comfort. Divine chrēstotēs (Rom 2:4) deepens conscience by making repentance both possible and compelling — God's goodness creates the conditions in which the conscience can function toward its proper end (turning). (OBS-067-OBS-021, OBS-067-OBS-035, OBS-067-OBS-043) |
| OBS-067-T2-076 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Eze 36:31, Mal 2:17, Rom 2:4 | The conscience engagement reveals that goodness functions as the calibrating standard for the conscience's operation. Conscience requires a standard against which it measures; goodness provides that standard. This means that the health of the conscience is tied to the health of the goodness-characteristic: a corrupted goodness standard produces a corrupted conscience witness; a genuine goodness standard enables a truthful conscience. The Eze 36:31 sequence — divine action producing moral self-recognition and loathing — suggests that the restoration of truthful conscience requires a prior restoration of the goodness standard. Goodness and conscience are structurally co-dependent: conscience witnesses accurately to the degree that the good it witnesses against is genuine. (OBS-067-OBS-021, OBS-067-OBS-035, SBF-067-Q049) |
| OBS-067-T2-084 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R067 goodness | Gen 1:31, Rom 11:22, Rom 2:4 | The evidence reveals a structural tension between the unconditional and conditional registers — and this tension is itself the analytical finding. On the unconditional side: Gen 1:31 establishes goodness as a creative-ontological act prior to any human response — God declares creation good without condition. The doxological refrain "for he is good, his steadfast love endures forever" names an enduring, non-contingent character attribute. On the conditional side: Rom 11:22 names a condition on the ongoing experience of divine chrēstotēs — "provided you continue in his kindness. Otherwise you too will be cut off." This is not a condition on God's character but on the human's ongoing reception of its benefits. Rom 2:4 implies responsiveness — God's kindness "is meant to lead you to repentance," with an implied expectation that repentance follows. The resolution: God's goodness as a character attribute is unconditional; the human's ongoing experience of its relational benefits is covenantal-responsive. This distinction is analytically significant and carries to SP-067-018 (the conditionality question). (OBS-067-OBS-006, OBS-067-OBS-043, OBS-067-OBS-044, SP-067-018) |
| OBS-067-T2-085 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R067 goodness | Gen 1:31, Jos 23:14, Lam 3:25, Eph 2:7 | God's extension of goodness reveals a disposition of initiatory, generous, and sustaining goodwill toward the human person. Three dimensions: (1) Initiatory — God acts first in goodness (Gen 1:31; Eph 2:7 — "so that in the coming ages he might show the immeasurable riches of his grace in kindness toward us in Christ Jesus"). The initiative is always divine. (2) Generous — chrēstotēs names not merely adequate goodness but generous goodwill (886-001): God's disposition is one of overflowing benevolence. (3) Sustaining — the covenantal promise (884-005, Jos 23:14: "not one word has failed") names a goodness that holds through time and maintains its commitment. The disposition is not reactive but proactive, generous, and reliable. Lam 3:25 ("the Lord is good to those who wait for him") names waiting as the human posture corresponding to this divine sustaining goodness — the relationship is not passive but involves a human receptive posture. (OBS-067-OBS-006, OBS-067-OBS-033, OBS-067-OBS-034, SBF-067-Q006) |
| OBS-067-T2-086 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R067 goodness | Psa 34:8, Lam 3:25, Psa 119:68 | Human-to-God operation is evidenced through three modes: (1) Doxological worship — the liturgical refrain "for he is good, his steadfast love endures forever" (group 884-001) is goodness operating as the content of the human's praise and confession toward God. The human declares God's goodness in worship — this is goodness moving upward from the human toward God as acknowledgement and adoration. (2) Seeking and trust — Psa 34:8 ("blessed is the man who takes refuge in him") and Lam 3:25 ("the soul who seeks him"): the human's movement toward God in trust and seeking is enabled by the encounter with divine goodness. Goodness produces the movement toward God. (3) Covenantal request — Psa 119:68 ("you are good and do good; teach me your statutes"): the human addresses God's goodness as the ground for petition. God's goodness is invoked as the basis of the human's request — "because you are good, teach me." (OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-026, OBS-067-OBS-033) |
| OBS-067-T2-087 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Lam 3:25, Psa 34:8, Psa 73:28 | Three inner postures are named for human-toward-God movement in goodness: (1) Waiting — Lam 3:25 ("the Lord is good to those who wait for him"). Waiting is the inner posture of active expectation without premature action — a stance of dependence and trust. (2) Seeking — Lam 3:25 ("the soul who seeks him"). Seeking is directional — the soul oriented actively toward God. (3) Trust and refuge — Psa 34:8 ("blessed is the man who takes refuge in him"): refuge-taking is the inner posture of surrender and reliance. These three postures share a common structure: they all involve the person relinquishing self-sufficiency and directing the inner being toward God. They contrast directly with the closing inner orientations (Q&A-032): self-direction, wilful refusal, and departure from faith. The enabling postures are fundamentally un-self-centred; the blocking postures are fundamentally self-centred. (OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-033, SBF-067-Q014) |
| OBS-067-T2-088 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R067 goodness | Psa 119:68, Psa 34:8, Psa 73:28 | The human-to-God direction reveals that the relationship is one of responsive dependence grounded in prior divine initiative. The sequence is always: God's goodness is encountered or declared first → the human moves toward God in trust, praise, and seeking → God sustains and fulfils. The human's movement toward God in goodness is not autonomous spiritual achievement but a response enabled by prior encounter with divine goodness. Psa 119:68's "you are good... teach me" names the relationship structure directly: God's goodness is the basis; human receptive orientation follows. The relationship revealed is one of: initiated by God, received by the human, responded to in trust and worship, sustained in covenantal fidelity. The human's part is not passive but it is always derivative — the human's goodness-orientation toward God is itself produced by God's prior goodness toward the human. (OBS-067-OBS-026, OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-001) |
| OBS-067-T2-091 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R067 goodness | Col 3:12, Rom 3:12, Psa 73:28 | The evidence requires that the giver must have: (1) Received Spirit-transformation — Rom 3:12 names the unregenerate condition as one of total goodness-absence. The implication is that genuine goodness-giving requires a prior Spirit-regeneration that creates the goodness-capacity. (2) Undergone moral reorientation — Psa 73:28's Asaph has moved from crisis to repositioned inner orientation before he can overflow goodness toward others. The person who has not undergone inner reorientation cannot genuinely give what they do not possess. (3) Been recognised as God's chosen and beloved — Col 3:12 explicitly grounds the "put on kindness" instruction in the prior identity declaration ("God's chosen ones, holy and beloved"). Giving genuine goodness requires the identity transformation that comes from being received by God in grace. This is the structural parallel: human goodness-giving to others requires the same prior divine-goodness-giving to the human that divine goodness-giving to creation requires nothing from creation (Gen 1:31 is unconditional). The human's giving is conditional on having first received. (OBS-067-OBS-039, OBS-067-OBS-046, OBS-067-OBS-029) |
| OBS-067-T2-093 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Jer 6:16, Rom 11:22 | The blocking conditions identified at Q&A-032 (self-direction, wilful refusal, moral redefinition, departure from faith) are stated primarily in the divine-to-human direction but logically apply also to human-to-human goodness reception: the self-directed person who refuses the good way (Jer 6:16) would resist receiving goodness from a human source just as from a divine one. The enabling conditions from Q&A-031 (nearness to God, fear of the Lord, Spirit-filling) apply most directly to divine reception. For human-to-human reception specifically: the data does not give a direct verse naming what enables or blocks receiving goodness from another person. This is a genuine partial gap — the blocking conditions are extrapolatable from the divine-direction evidence, but explicit verse evidence for the human-to-human receiving conditions is absent. (OBS-067-OBS-028, OBS-067-OBS-043) |
| OBS-067-T2-094 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Jer 6:16, Est 5:9 | The verse evidence addresses non-reception primarily in the divine-to-human direction. Jer 6:16 ("we will not walk in it") is the clearest picture: the inner state is one of offered-and-refused goodness — active resistance of what is genuinely good. The will has been engaged and has chosen refusal. The person's inner state is: morally informed (they have encountered the good way), volitionally closed (they have refused it), and unaware of the full consequence of their refusal. For the human-to-human non-reception context specifically: the data is silent on the inner state of a person who encounters human goodness and refuses it. The Haman narrative (Est 5:9) names the inner state of the person who misidentifies goodness — but this is self-deception rather than active refusal of another's goodness. This is a partial gap. (OBS-067-OBS-040, OBS-067-OBS-043) |
| OBS-067-T2-096 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R067 goodness | Gen 1:31, Mic 6:8, Rom 2:4, Jos 23:14 | The evidence supports both covenantal and cross-covenantal operation. Within covenantal contexts: 884-005 (God's covenantal word, Jos 23:14), 886-001 and 886-002 (Spirit-produced goodness in the new covenant community). These are explicitly covenantal. Cross-covenantal: H2896A in 884-002's moral character register applies to human moral life broadly — the prophetic verdict on not-good conduct (884-006) is addressed to covenant people but the ethical standard (Mic 6:8) has a universal logic. The creation-goodness of Gen 1:31 (884-007) is pre-covenantal — God's goodness extends to all creation before any covenant is formed. Rom 2:4 ("God's kindness leads you to repentance") is addressed to those outside the covenant community as well as within — the mechanism of divine chrēstotēs is not restricted to the covenant. (OBS-067-OBS-006, OBS-067-OBS-036, OBS-067-OBS-043) |
| OBS-067-T2-097 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R067 goodness | Gen 1:31, Rom 2:4, Rom 11:22, Lam 3:25 | The relational scope of goodness is wide but not fully mapped in the data. Inclusion evidenced: creation as a whole (Gen 1:31 — all that God made is good); covenant community (Jos 23:14; Col 3:12; Rom 15:14); those outside the covenant who receive divine chrēstotēs leading to repentance (Rom 2:4); the soul who seeks (Lam 3:25 — the seeking person, without boundary). Possible exclusion: Rom 11:22 names "those who have fallen" as the recipients of severity rather than chrēstotēs — but this is presented as a relational condition rather than a categorical exclusion. The data does not name any class of persons categorically excluded from the scope of divine goodness; the blocking conditions (Q&A-032) describe inner orientations that close reception, not divine decisions to exclude. Whether the human expression of goodness has specific scope limits is not directly addressed. Partial gap. (OBS-067-OBS-006, OBS-067-OBS-043, OBS-067-OBS-044) |
| OBS-067-T2-098 | OBSERVATION | R067 goodness | N/A | The distortion evidence (chrēstologia — G5542 as the etymological distortion form; Mal 2:17 — calling evil good; Jer 44:17 — redefining goodness as material advantage) names distortion as a pattern. Whether this distortion is explicitly linked to adversarial spiritual activity is not directly addressed in the anchor verses. The distortion appears in the data as a human phenomenon (self-justification, inverted moral assessment) rather than an explicitly adversarial spiritual attack. The possibility that goodness is a site of adversarial attack is plausible from the pattern of distortion but is not evidenced in the anchor verses. This is a genuine partial gap. (OBS-067-OBS-021, OBS-067-OBS-024, SBF-067-Q065) |
| OBS-067-T2-100 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Rom 11:22 | The evidence raises reversibility as a concern without fully resolving it. Rom 11:22 names a conditional dimension: "provided you continue in his kindness. Otherwise you too will be cut off." The use of "cut off" implies that the relational experience of divine goodness can be lost — which implies some form of reversibility in the human's receipt of goodness's benefits. However, this addresses the ongoing experience of chrēstotēs rather than the Spirit-level character transformation. Whether Spirit-produced goodness (agathōsunē and chrēstotēs as fruit) can be reversed is not directly addressed. The distinction between the character transformation (Spirit-produced inner quality) and the relational experience (ongoing receipt of divine chrēstotēs) is analytically important for this question, and the data does not fully resolve it. This is a genuine partial gap — formal vs genuine receipt distinction is not drawn in the evidence. (OBS-067-OBS-043, OBS-067-OBS-044, SP-067-018) |
| OBS-067-T2-101 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Psa 34:8, Psa 73:28, Eze 36:31, Rom 2:4 | Two distinct sequences are named. Sequence 1 — positive transformation arc (divine goodness → reception → reorientation): (a) Encounter with divine goodness — Psa 34:8 ("taste and see"); (b) Trust and refuge — "blessed is the man who takes refuge in him"; (c) Stable inner orientation — Psa 73:28 ("it is good to be near God; I have made the Lord God my refuge"); (d) Overflow toward others — "that I may tell of all your works" (Psa 73:28 conclusion). Sequence 2 — moral awareness arc (recognition → repentance → transformation): (a) Memory of deeds — Eze 36:31 ("you will remember your evil ways"); (b) Recognition as not-good — "and your deeds that were not good"; (c) Self-loathing — "you will loathe yourselves"; (d) Movement toward repentance — implied by context (Eze 36:26 God's Spirit given follows 36:31's loathing). Rom 2:4 names the divine chrēstotēs → repentance sequence directly. Both sequences are coherent and traceable in the verse evidence. (OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-035, OBS-067-OBS-029, SBF-067-Q031) |
| OBS-067-T2-102 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Psa 34:8, Psa 73:28, Eze 36:31 | The two sequences reveal that goodness works through different mechanisms depending on its register. The positive arc (encounter → trust → reorientation → overflow) reveals that goodness works by producing a progressive deepening of inner orientation — encounter leads to trust, trust leads to stable orientation, orientation leads to outward expression. The characteristic builds from first contact to sustained character. The moral awareness arc (memory → recognition → loathing → repentance) reveals that goodness works through the confrontation with its own absence — the standard of the good, when encountered, produces awareness of not-goodness that is itself a mechanism of transformation. Goodness therefore works both through positive encounter (receiving divine goodness) and through negative recognition (confronting one's absence of goodness). The sequence also reveals the role of time: both arcs are extended through time, not instantaneous — goodness is a formative characteristic, not a momentary experience. (OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-029, OBS-067-OBS-035) |
| OBS-067-T2-105 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Lam 3:25, Psa 34:8, Psa 73:28 | Yes — the goodness-suffering connection is evidenced in two registers (SBF-067-Q048): (1) Divine goodness sustaining in affliction — Lam 3:25 ("the Lord is good to those who wait for him, to the soul who seeks him") is composed in the context of Lamentations — the most concentrated suffering in the Hebrew canon. God's goodness is named as accessible and sustaining precisely in the condition of profound affliction. Suffering does not negate goodness; it becomes the context in which waiting for God's goodness is practised and discovered. (2) Divine goodness available in crisis — Psa 34:8 ("taste and see that the Lord is good") is written by David in the context of his distress — Psalm 34 is a crisis psalm. The invitation to taste goodness is issued from within suffering. The goodness-suffering connection in R67 is not incidental — it is structurally embedded in the anchor verse contexts. (OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-033, SBF-067-Q048) |
| OBS-067-T2-106 | VERSE_PATTERN | R067 goodness | Psa 73:28, Lam 3:25, Est 5:9, Eze 36:31 | The evidence supports all four modes but with different emphases. Deepening: Psa 73:28 — Asaph's suffering (crisis of doubt, seeing the prosperity of the wicked, Psa 73:1–16) precedes and produces his reorientation toward God as the supreme good. The suffering is the condition that makes the goodness of nearness to God visible by contrast. The characteristic is deepened by the suffering — Asaph discovers what is truly good precisely through the crisis. Revealing: suffering reveals that goodness rooted in God is resistant to circumstantial disruption (Psa 73:28 — stable inner orientation despite unchanged circumstances); it also reveals the fragility of goodness not so rooted (Est 5:9 — Haman's gladness destroyed instantly). Testing: Lam 3:25 — waiting for God's goodness in the context of catastrophic national suffering is a test of whether the person's confidence in goodness is genuine. Producing: the Eze 36:31 sequence (suffering of exile → moral reckoning → repentance) suggests that suffering can be the context in which goodness is ultimately produced through the confrontation with its own absence. (OBS-067-OBS-029, OBS-067-OBS-033, OBS-067-OBS-041) |

| OBS-067-T2-133 | ETYMOLOGY | R067 goodness | Eze 36:31, Rom 3:12 | Yes — three forms. (1) Direct negation: H2896A used with negative particle — "not good" (Group 884-006, 22 verses). The vocabulary generates its own opposite through simple negation rather than a separate root. (2) Universal absence: chrēstotēs used in its negative in Rom 3:12 ("no one does good, not even one" — from Ps 14:3 LXX). The same term names the characteristic and its complete absence. (3) Etymological distortion: G5542 (chrēstologia — smooth talk) is the chrēsto root's distortion form — the outward language of goodness without its substance. The distortion-opposite is lexically related, not a separate root. (OBS-067-OBS-021, OBS-067-OBS-049, SBF-067-Q021) |

| OBS-067-T2-139 | VERSE_ANNOTATION | R067 goodness | Mic 6:8 | Mic 6:8 as primary definitional anchor: H2896A (tov — good) functions as the predicate of the divine definition: "He has told you, O man, what is good." Tov here is the predicate nominative of the divine declaration — it names the content of what God has revealed. The term functions as: (1) The criterion of divine requirement ("what does the Lord require of you but..."); (2) The content of revelation ("He has told you what is good"); (3) The implicit standard against which the three-fold expression (justice, kindness, humility) is measured. The primary term in the primary verse is definitionally and revelatorily placed — it is the named object of divine instruction to the human person. (OBS-067-OBS-027, SBF-067-Q042) |
| OBS-067-T2-141 | VERSE_ANNOTATION | R067 goodness | Mic 6:8, Rom 2:4, Rom 11:22 | Three key argument structures: (1) Mic 6:8 — definitional argument: if goodness is defined as X, and X consists of justice + kindness + humility, then goodness is not a simple quality but a tri-fold orientation. The logical structure is definitional-analytical. (2) Rom 2:4 — teleological argument: God's chrēstotēs has a purpose ("meant to lead you to repentance") — the argument is that the purpose of divine goodness is the human's turning. The logical structure is purposive. (3) Rom 11:22 — conditional argument: God's kindness operates under a conditional that requires human continuance ("provided you continue in his kindness"). The logical structure is conditional. These three argument structures (definitional, purposive, conditional) represent three different logical relationships between goodness and other inner-being realities. (OBS-067-OBS-027, OBS-067-OBS-043, OBS-067-OBS-044, SBF-067-Q042) |
| OBS-067-T2-143 | VERSE_ANNOTATION | R067 goodness | Mic 6:8, Psa 119:68 | Mic 6:8 is the primary anchor for human goodness in its most comprehensive definitional statement: "He has told you, O man, what is good; and what does the Lord require of you but to do justice, and to love kindness, and to walk humbly with your God?" It functions as: (1) Revelatory — goodness is defined as divinely disclosed, not humanly generated; (2) Definitional — it names the content (justice + kindness + humility); (3) Comprehensive — it covers horizontal (justice, kindness) and vertical (humble walk with God) dimensions simultaneously; (4) Cross-registry — it is the highest-density shared anchor in the programme (6 co-anchoring registries). Psa 119:68 functions as the primary anchor for the divine-human correspondence structure of goodness: "You are good and do good" — the being/doing model at the divine level. (OBS-067-OBS-027, SBF-067-Q042, SBF-067-Q138) |
| OBS-067-T2-144 | VERSE_ANNOTATION | R067 goodness | Mic 6:8 | Mic 6:8 uniquely reveals: (1) That goodness is divinely revealed and humanly required — "He has told you" positions goodness as knowledge given from God to the human, and "the Lord requires" positions it as covenantal obligation, not optional aspiration. No other verse in the anchor set places goodness simultaneously as revealed knowledge and covenantal requirement. (2) That goodness as a human characteristic is internally structured — justice, kindness, and humble walk are not three separate characteristics but the tri-fold content of what it means to be good. No other verse provides this internal structure. (3) That goodness requires comprehensive orientation — both horizontal (toward others) and vertical (toward God) — simultaneously. Psa 73:28 focuses on the vertical; Rom 15:14 on the horizontal; Mic 6:8 holds both together as constitutive of goodness. This is the verse's unique analytical contribution. (OBS-067-OBS-027, SBF-067-Q042) |
| OBS-067-T2-148 | OBSERVATION | R067 goodness | Psa 73:28, Col 3:12 | The developmental psychology framework surfaces a question not fully addressed: does goodness develop in stages within the individual inner being, and if so, what are the stage markers? | SYN-INTRA-067-001 | SYNTHESIS_INTRA_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T1 as a whole reveals that goodness is constitutively multi-modal but unified in origin. The characteristic's 12 distinct operational modes (Q&A-022) span quality, disposition, condition, and evaluative act — and yet all share a single definitional structure: goodness is what proceeds from and returns to God. The immediate response varies by encounter type (Q&A-025-026), the sustained effect is constitutive rather than reactive (Q&A-030), and the dimension structure is bipartite — Moral Character for human-subject goodness, Divine-Human Correspondence for divine-subject (Q&A-034). What the tier as a whole reveals is that the apparent definitional complexity of goodness (12 modes, multiple dimensions, varied immediate responses) resolves into a coherent characterological portrait: goodness is the quality of alignment with the character of the one who is Good — variable in its expression but unified in its source and standard. |
| SYN-INTRA-067-002 | SYNTHESIS_INTRA_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T2 as a whole reveals that goodness has a comprehensive multi-level constitutional presence — from Spirit-level origin through soul-orientation, heart-engagement, and mind-evaluative functions to outward expression — but with a marked asymmetry in evidence quality. Spirit-level (Q&A-037-039), soul-level (Q&A-040-041), and heart-level (Q&A-043-044) are all substantively evidenced. Mind-level is partially evidenced through cognitive dimensions. Body-level is thin — the gladness-of-heart compound (tov-lev) and the taste-and-see perceptual metaphor are the somatic markers, but no constitutional body deposit is evidenced (T2.8 silent). The most structurally significant T2 finding is the six-step constitutional movement (Q&A-064): Spirit-level origin → soul reorientation → heart engagement → volitional alignment → agentive resolve → outward expression. This movement is top-down and outward, with no evidenced reversal from body to inner being. Goodness is constitutionally deep but does not leave a somatic deposit. |
| SYN-INTRA-067-003 | SYNTHESIS_INTRA_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T3 reveals that goodness is the most comprehensively faculty-engaging characteristic in the programme's current analytical record. Every faculty except creativity (T3.5 — silence confirmed) shows substantive engagement: perception (tasting and seeing goodness), cognition (moral evaluation and ranking), memory (covenantal knowing and moral reckoning), affect (stable rooted gladness vs fragile surface gladness), volition (reconstituted toward the good), agency (active expression required and enabled), moral evaluation (goodness as the normative anchor of the evaluative faculty), conscience (goodness calibrates the conscience's standard), conscientiousness (integration of all faculties into sustained moral expression), and relational capacity (dual-directional overflow). The tier as a whole reveals that goodness is not a specialist characteristic — it is an integrating one. The person who possesses genuine goodness has every faculty engaged and aligned. This comprehensive faculty-engagement is what distinguishes genuine goodness from its surface forms: surface goodness (the Haman problem) engages affect without engaging conscience or volitional alignment; genuine goodness integrates all. |
| SYN-INTRA-067-004 | SYNTHESIS_INTRA_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T4 as a whole reveals that goodness operates across all relational directions simultaneously — God-to-human, human-to-God, and human-to-human — but with a fundamental asymmetry: the God-to-human direction is prior, generative, and abundant in evidence; the human-to-God direction is responsive and derivative; the human-to-human direction is overflow from prior reception. This is not a simple tripartite scheme but a structured cascade: God's goodness initiates and flows toward the human person (Q&A-099-101); the human responds in trust, seeking, and worship (Q&A-103-105); the goodness received overflows toward others through instruction, justice, and kindness (Q&A-107-109). The most structurally significant T4 finding is the unconditional/conditional distinction (Q&A-100): God's goodness as a character attribute is unconditional; the human's ongoing experience of its relational benefits is conditional on continued trust. This distinction has direct implications for how goodness is taught, received, and sustained. The spiritual beings interface is largely silent — the relational architecture of goodness is thoroughly human-divine in orientation. |
| SYN-INTRA-067-005 | SYNTHESIS_INTRA_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T5 as a whole reveals that goodness is one of the most developmentally rich characteristics in the programme. Three transformation types (condition change, orientation change, character transformation — Q&A-123), two distinct transformation sequences (positive encounter arc and moral awareness arc — Q&A-126), and three context-differentiated transformation mechanisms (encounter, Spirit-infusion, recognition-confrontation — Q&A-129) together produce a portrait of a characteristic that reaches the person through multiple developmental pathways depending on their condition and context. The formation/sanctification section confirms that goodness plays a dual developmental role: it is a product of formation (Spirit-formed character) and an agent of formation (the good person enables the formation of others — Q&A-136). The relationship to suffering deepens this: suffering is not incidental but a context in which goodness is deepened, revealed, tested, and even produced through confrontation with its absence (Q&A-133). T5.7 closes formally — no somatic deposit evidenced. The eschatological trajectory is present but underdeveloped in the data (Q&A-138-139). |
| SYN-INTRA-067-006 | SYNTHESIS_INTRA_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T6 as a whole reveals that goodness is structurally one of the most connected characteristics in the programme — both in co-occurrence breadth (top-25 co-occurrence list spanning the full dimensional spectrum), shared anchor density (7 shared anchors including the two highest-density synthesis nodes in the programme: Mic 6:8 and Gal 5:22), and root-level vocabulary sharing across three root families (TOV, AGATHO, CHRESTO) distributed across multiple registries. The structural picture confirms what T1 indicated definitionally: goodness is not a specialist characteristic but one that permeates and connects the whole inner-being landscape. The most significant T6 finding is the dual constitutive relationship (Q&A-152): goodness is simultaneously a constituent of the Spirit-fruit whole (Gal 5:22) and itself constituted by a tri-fold structure (Mic 6:8). This means goodness has both a containing relationship (it is part of something larger — Spirit-formed character) and an internal structure (it contains three orientations — justice, kindness, humility). The boundary distinctions (T6.5) remain the weakest component — all three nearest-neighbour boundaries (love, kindness, generosity) require Session D cross-registry synthesis. The R187 (strength) co-occurrence anomaly at 32 shared verses remains an open question for Session D (SP-067-027). |
| SYN-INTRA-067-007 | SYNTHESIS_INTRA_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T7 as a whole reveals a characteristic whose evidential foundation is both exceptionally strong in some dimensions and genuinely constrained in others. Lexically: three OWNER terms across two languages with distinct semantic ranges, a full root-family analysis, a NT coinage (agathōsunē), and a comprehensive vocabulary arc from phenomenological breadth (H2896A) to virtuous precision (agathōsunē) to relational precision (chrēstotēs — Q&A-177). The lexical evidence is rich. Literary: the primary anchor (Mic 6:8) uniquely combines revelatory and covenantal-obligation frames with an internal tri-fold structure that no other verse replicates (Q&A-183); the anchor corpus spans prophetic, wisdom, psalmic, and epistolary forms across multiple contextual settings (Q&A-181). Human science: virtue ethics is the closest parallel framework, with the critical divergence at the source of goodness (divine gift vs natural achievement — Q&A-186). Constraints: NO_WORD_ANALYSIS flags on both Greek OWNER terms limit the LXX analysis (Q&A-175); LSJ data is absent. The evidential foundation is strong at the lexical-semantic and verse-literary levels; it is constrained at the LXX-continuity and human-science elaboration levels. This asymmetry is itself an analytic finding — it identifies where future investigation would most strengthen the registry's evidential base. |
| SYN-INTER-067-008 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | What T1 named (goodness as primarily a quality — a settled characterological property) is confirmed and located by T2. The constitutional movement identified in T2 (six-step: Spirit → soul → heart → volition → agency → expression) explains how a characterological quality (T1's finding) is not a static property but a dynamic disposition that moves through the person from its deepest level outward. T1's four constituent poles (ontological, moral-character, experiential, volitional) map directly onto T2's constitutional levels: the ontological pole operates at Spirit-level origin; the moral-character pole at soul and heart; the experiential pole at soul-seeking; the volitional pole at the volitional step. The two tiers read together reveal that goodness is a quality that is simultaneously constitutionally located and constitutionally mobile — it has its deepest root at the Spirit level and its expression at the social-ethical level. |
| SYN-INTER-067-009 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T1 identified goodness as an integrating characteristic with 12 modes of operation. T3 confirms the mechanism of integration: goodness is integrating because it engages and aligns every inner faculty simultaneously. The T1 finding that goodness has a communicative mode (doxological declaration and divine revelatory speech — Q&A-024) connects to T3's perceptual (Q&A-066) and relational (Q&A-096) faculty findings: the communicative mode of goodness engages perception (tasting and seeing), activates conscience (the divine word naming what is good calibrates the conscience's standard), and builds relational capacity (community-forming declaration). The T3 finding that goodness is fundamentally integrating (Q&A-095) is what makes its T1 multi-modality coherent: the 12 modes are not fragmented operations but facets of a single faculty-integrating reality. |
| SYN-INTER-067-010 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T1 named goodness as carrying directional, relational, and constitutional implications in its very name (Q&A-015). T4 maps those implications onto the relational architecture: the directional implication (goodness flows from God and toward others) is precisely the God-to-human cascade confirmed in T4; the relational implication (goodness operates in relationship) is confirmed by the extensive T4 evidence across all three relational directions; the constitutional implication (goodness is a character depth, not a surface act) is confirmed by the T4 finding that human-to-human giving of goodness requires prior constitutional transformation (Q&A-109). T1's naming-level insight (Q&A-015) is validated by T4's relational-structure evidence: the name carries the architecture. |
| SYN-INTER-067-011 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T1's distinction between immediate response and sustained effect (Q&A-030) maps precisely onto T5's two transformation sequences (Q&A-126). The immediate response pattern (trust, praise, receptive desire) corresponds to the initial stages of T5's positive arc (encounter → trust → refuge). The sustained effect (stable orientation, community capacity, settled disposition) corresponds to T5's later stages (reorientation → overflow → formation-agent capacity). Furthermore, T1's "sustained effect" (Q&A-028) identified stable inner orientation as the primary sustained product — T5 confirms this through the transformation to orientation change (Q&A-123) and the formation-agent role (Q&A-136). The two tiers read together reveal a temporal architecture for goodness: immediate contact produces first responses; sustained formation produces constitutive character; and the character-formed person becomes a formative resource for others. |
| SYN-INTER-067-012 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T1's boundary analysis (Q&A-019-021) identified three structural opposites and named the boundary problem with adjacent characteristics as partially unresolved. T6's structural relationships data confirms and deepens this: the boundary between goodness and love (R103), kindness (R99), and generosity (R65) is structurally unresolved at the root level — the vocabulary sharing (T6.4) and co-occurrence data (T6.1) reveal that these registries are adjacent precisely because they share root families and co-anchor at the same verses. T1's definition of goodness as constituted by the Mic 6:8 tri-fold (justice + kindness + humility — Q&A-152) directly names kindness as a component of goodness — which means the goodness-kindness boundary (T1.3 partially answered, T6.5 partially answered) is not between two separate characteristics but between a whole and one of its constituents. This reading resolves the boundary partially: kindness may be a constituent mode of goodness rather than a peer characteristic. Session D testing required. |
| SYN-INTER-067-013 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T1 identified that goodness is complex in structure — the name "goodness" does not capture the semantic breadth of the vocabulary (Q&A-013). T7 confirms and explains this complexity through the vocabulary arc (Q&A-177): the three-stage development (H2896A's phenomenological breadth → agathōsunē's virtuous precision → chrēstotēs's relational precision) corresponds precisely to T1's finding that goodness operates across quality, disposition, condition, and evaluative act (Q&A-016). Each stage of the vocabulary arc covers a different aspect of T1's definitional complexity: H2896A covers quality, condition, and evaluative act; agathōsunē covers the character-quality and virtuous-resolve aspect; chrēstotēs covers the dispositional-relational aspect. T7 thus provides the lexical grounding for T1's multi-modal portrait — the vocabulary complexity matches the definitional complexity. |
| SYN-INTER-067-014 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T2 identified the constitutional movement of goodness from Spirit-level through soul and heart to outward expression (six-step sequence, Q&A-064). T3 names the faculty operations at each level: Spirit-level corresponds to Spirit-production (T2.1) → T3.6 volitional reconstitution (the Spirit enables the resolve for good); soul-level seeking (T2.2) → T3.1 perceptual encounter (tasting and seeing); heart-level knowing and gladness (T2.3) → T3.3 memory and T3.4 affect; evaluative-cognitive function (T2.4) → T3.2 cognition and T3.8 moral evaluation; outward expression (T2.10) → T3.7 agency and T3.11 relational capacity. The two tiers together reveal that the constitutional movement is not an abstract structural description but a faculty-by-faculty account of how goodness moves through the person. Each constitutional level corresponds to specific faculty engagements. The T3 faculty map gives content to the T2 constitutional architecture. |
| SYN-INTER-067-015 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T2 confirmed that goodness originates externally to the person — the origin is God's character, Spirit, and word; the person is the site of reception, not generation (Q&A-060-061). T4 maps the relational architecture of this origin: the God-to-human direction (T4.1) is the relational channel through which the external origin reaches the person; the human-to-God direction (T4.2) is the responsive movement that the received goodness enables; the human-to-human direction (T4.3) is the overflow once received. T2's single-origin/multiple-mechanism finding (Q&A-061) maps onto T4's three relational directions: the encounter mechanism corresponds to the human-to-God direction (seeking and tasting); the covenantal-word mechanism corresponds to the God-to-human covenantal direction; the Spirit-infusion mechanism is the Spirit's work in the human constitutional levels (T2.1-2.2). The constitutional and relational architectures are co-extensive — they describe the same reality from inside (constitutional) and outside (relational). |
| SYN-INTER-067-016 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T2's constitutional movement (six-step sequence) and T5's transformation sequences (positive arc and moral awareness arc) describe the same developmental reality from two analytical perspectives. T2 describes where goodness moves in the person (constitutional levels); T5 describes how goodness moves the person (mechanisms and sequences). The six-step T2 sequence (Spirit → soul → heart → volition → agency → expression) is the constitutional substrate through which T5's transformation arcs operate. T5's three mechanisms (encounter, Spirit-infusion, recognition-confrontation) are the relational/contextual triggers that activate the T2 constitutional movement. Together: the T2 constitutional architecture provides the vertical structure through which T5's developmental arcs run. Formation is not a disembodied process — it is the progressive deepening of goodness through the constitutional levels identified in T2. |
| SYN-INTER-067-017 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T2 confirmed that goodness's origin is singular (God) but its delivery is multiple (encounter, word, Spirit). T6 confirmed that goodness's vocabulary is distributed across multiple registries through shared root families. These two findings are structurally related: the vocabulary distribution (T6.4 — TOV root to R103, AGATHO root to R65, CHRESTO root to R99) reflects the multiple delivery mechanisms of T2. The TOV root's distribution to love (R103) corresponds to the encounter-relational delivery mechanism; the AGATHO root's distribution to generosity (R65) corresponds to the action-expression level of the constitutional movement; the CHRESTO root's shared presence across R67 and R99 corresponds to the dispositional-kindness register of chrēstotēs. The vocabulary distribution is not arbitrary — it tracks the constitutional and relational architecture of how goodness operates and reaches the person. The single-origin of goodness (T2.9.2) is expressed through a vocabulary distributed across the programme's relational-characteristic cluster, reflecting the multiple pathways of the one goodness. |
| SYN-INTER-067-018 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T2's Spirit-level location finding (Q&A-037-039) — that genuine goodness requires Spirit-level intervention and is not naturally generated from within the person — is confirmed and enriched by T7's vocabulary arc (Q&A-177). The NT coinage of agathōsunē (Q&A-176) as a Spirit-fruit term reflects precisely the T2 finding: the NT needed a new term to name goodness at the Spirit-production level because the existing vocabulary (H2896A, agathos) covered the full semantic range without isolating the Spirit-produced inner virtue. The coinage is the lexical response to a constitutional reality (Spirit-level goodness as distinct from natural goodness). T7's evidential strength in lexical analysis and T2's constitutional depth together explain why the NT created agathōsunē: to name a constitutional phenomenon (Spirit-produced inner virtue at the deepest level of the person) that the existing vocabulary did not isolate with sufficient precision. |
| SYN-INTER-067-019 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T3 confirmed that goodness engages every inner faculty (except creativity), with particular depth in moral evaluation (goodness as the normative anchor — Q&A-089), conscience (goodness calibrates the standard — Q&A-092), and relational capacity (dual-directional overflow — Q&A-098). T4 confirmed that goodness operates across all three relational directions. The inter-tier connection: the faculty engagements of T3 are the inner mechanisms through which T4's relational directions operate. The God-to-human direction (T4.1) engages the perceptive faculty (tasting and seeing — Q&A-066) and the moral evaluation faculty (calibrating the conscience's standard — Q&A-092). The human-to-God direction (T4.2) engages the affective faculty (trust, praise) and the relational capacity faculty. The human-to-human direction (T4.3) engages agency (doing justice — Q&A-084) and relational capacity (overflow toward others — Q&A-098). T3's faculty map is the inner architecture; T4's relational directions are the external expressions of those faculties in lived relationship. They are not separate dimensions but the inside and outside of the same relational-characterological phenomenon. |
| SYN-INTER-067-020 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T3 identified three faculty engagements that are particularly significant for transformation: memory (Eze 36:31 — memory activates moral recognition, Q&A-072), affect (stable vs fragile gladness diagnostic, Q&A-077), and moral evaluation (conscience calibration, Q&A-089-090). T5's three transformation mechanisms (encounter, Spirit-infusion, recognition-confrontation — Q&A-129) map onto these faculty engagements. The recognition-confrontation mechanism (Eze 36:31 / Rom 2:4) operates specifically through the memory faculty (recall of deeds) and the conscience-moral evaluation faculty (recognition as not-good and self-loathing). The encounter mechanism operates through the perceptive faculty (tasting — Q&A-066) and the affective faculty (trust, gladness — Q&A-075). The Spirit-infusion mechanism produces faculty-level transformation: it reconstitutes the volitional faculty (Q&A-082), enables agency (Q&A-085), and generates stable affect (Q&A-076). T5's mechanisms are faculty-specific operations — each mechanism transforms the person by engaging specific faculties in specific ways. This is the most analytically precise inter-tier connection so far. |
| SYN-INTER-067-021 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T3 confirmed that goodness is fundamentally integrating — it engages and aligns every faculty (Q&A-095). T6 confirmed that goodness is centrally connected across the programme's characteristic landscape — broad co-occurrence, shared anchors at the highest-density synthesis nodes, root-vocabulary sharing across three families. These two findings are structurally related: a characteristic that is internally integrating (T3) is also externally connecting (T6). The Mic 6:8 anchor (T6.6 — shared with 6 registries) integrates justice, kindness, love, humility, compassion, and will in a single verse — this is the structural expression of goodness's integrating faculty-engagement. Gal 5:22 (shared with 5 registries) integrates Spirit-fruit characteristics in a cluster — the same integrating logic. The characteristic that most fully integrates the inner faculties (T3) is also the characteristic most structurally connected to adjacent characteristics (T6). Integration within produces connection without. |
| SYN-INTER-067-022 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T3's finding that goodness has an inherently perceptual character — it must be encountered and tasted, not merely inferred (Q&A-068) — is confirmed and grounded by T7's lexical and literary analysis. The Psa 34:8 primary verse functions literarily as an invitation to perceptual encounter (Q&A-178) — "taste and see" is the literary form that corresponds to the T3 perceptual faculty engagement. The phenomenological root of H2896A (Q&A-168 — begins with concrete experience of what is agreeable and beneficial, rises to moral categories) explains why T3's perceptive faculty is so prominently engaged: the Hebrew root of goodness is itself experiential-perceptual before it is ethical-conceptual. T7's etymological grounding explains the T3 perceptual finding: the vocabulary encodes the experiential mode of knowing goodness that T3's faculty analysis confirms. |
| SYN-INTER-067-023 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T4 identified that God's goodness as a character attribute is unconditional while the human's ongoing experience of its relational benefits is conditional (Q&A-100). T5 named the transformation arcs through which the conditional dimension operates: the positive transformation arc (encounter → trust → reorientation → overflow) is the arc in which the conditional relational experience is deepened; the moral awareness arc (recognition → loathing → repentance) is the arc that recovers the relational experience when it has been lost through departure. T4's human-to-God enabling inner postures (waiting, seeking, trust — Q&A-104) correspond to T5's formation mechanisms: the posture of waiting corresponds to the encounter mechanism (sustained receptive openness); the moral awareness arc is the recovery mechanism for the person who has departed from the relational posture. Together: T5's formative processes are the mechanisms through which T4's relational conditions are entered, sustained, and restored. Transformation and relationship are inseparable in goodness. |
| SYN-INTER-067-024 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T4 confirmed that goodness operates across all three relational directions (God-to-human, human-to-God, human-to-human). T6's structural relationships and shared anchors data confirms that the cross-registry connections of goodness are organised along the same relational axes. The Mic 6:8 shared anchor (6 co-registries) is organized along the human-to-God and human-to-human axes: humility with God (vertical) and justice/kindness with others (horizontal). The Gal 5:22 shared anchor (5 co-registries) is organized along the human-community axis: Spirit-fruit characteristics that are all expressed in human relationship. The sequential finding (goodness → repentance, goodness → faith — Q&A-147-148) corresponds to the God-to-human relational direction: divine goodness producing these inner-being responses in the human. T6's cross-registry connections are the structural record of goodness's relational reach — the registries it is most connected to are the registries it most closely interacts with in its relational operation. Relational architecture (T4) and structural position (T6) are co-extensive. |
| SYN-INTER-067-025 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T4 confirmed that the God-to-human direction of goodness is initiatory, generous, and sustaining (Q&A-101). T7 confirms that the primary literary form carrying divine goodness (prophetic covenantal address at Mic 6:8, doxological Psalm declarations, Pauline epistolary) all use forms that emphasise divine initiative and human receptivity. T7's literary analysis (Q&A-178-179) reveals that the literary forms carrying goodness are theologically intentional: the covenant lawsuit form (Mic 6:8) establishes divine initiative in defining the good; the invitation form ("taste and see" — Psa 34:8) establishes divine initiative in the encounter; the indicative-imperative structure of Col 3:12 (God has made you chosen → therefore put on kindness) establishes divine prior action before human response. T7's literary analysis confirms T4's relational architecture through the literary forms that carry them: the literature itself models the God-initiates-human-responds structure. |
| SYN-INTER-067-026 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T5 confirmed that goodness plays a dual developmental role — formation product and formation agent (Q&A-136). T6 confirmed that the characteristics most closely connected to goodness in co-occurrence and shared anchors are the same characteristics named in the transformation arcs. The Gal 5:22 shared anchors (love, joy, patience, peace, faith) are the characteristics that co-occur in the context of Spirit-produced formation (the fruit list). The Mic 6:8 shared anchors (justice, kindness, love, humility) are the characteristics that are co-constituted with goodness in the definition of the formed human moral life. The cross-registry connections (T6) are the structural record of what characteristics goodness produces in others when it acts as a formation agent (T5). R103 (love) as the highest co-occurrence connection (T6.1 — 28 shared verses) is also the characteristic most naturally produced by encounter with goodness in the positive transformation arc — goodness → trust and love as natural co-products. T5's formation-agent role has T6's cross-registry connections as its structural trace in the programme. |
| SYN-INTER-067-027 | SYNTHESIS_INTER_TIER | R067 goodness |  | T5 named three transformation mechanisms (encounter, Spirit-infusion, recognition-confrontation) operating across three contextual registers (experiential/worship, pneumatological-community, prophetic/apostolic). T7 confirms that these three mechanisms correspond to three of the four literary forms carrying the anchor verse evidence: the encounter mechanism is carried by Psalms (experiential, invitational literature — Q&A-178-179); the Spirit-infusion mechanism is carried by NT epistolary (pneumatological instruction); the recognition-confrontation mechanism is carried by prophetic literature (covenant lawsuit and moral address). The literary form carries the transformation mechanism appropriate to it. T7's literary analysis (Q&A-179) also confirms T5's formation/sanctification finding (Q&A-135): the "fruit" metaphor of Gal 5:22 is an agricultural-developmental image embedded in a paraenetic (instructional-formative) letter — the literary form confirms that formation is the intent. T7's evidential foundation illuminates why T5's transformation mechanisms are distributed as they are: the mechanisms are embedded in literary forms designed to activate them. |

| DIM-15-001 | DIMENSION_REVIEW | R015 boastfulness |  | The ka.vod (glory) vocabulary in Reg 15 (boastfulness) contains its own internal corrective: divine glory (1861-001) as the proper object of inner awe, and the human act of directing glory toward God (1861-002) as the antidote to boastfulness that claims glory for itself. Session B should examine how the glory vocabulary frames the proper and improper objects of inner honour — why the same word (kavod) names both human boastfulness and divine majesty, and what this reveals about the inner-being structure of worship. |
| DIM-187-001 | DIMENSION_REVIEW | R187 strength | 1 Sam 15:22 | Group 7013-001 (a.yil, ram) carries a verse-level valuation in which obedience (H8085H sha.ma) and attentive listening (H7181 qashav) are declared greater than sacrifice — greater than the fat of rams (1 Sam 15:22). Neither term is an OWNER in Registry 187; their inner-being weight is present in the verse but unregistered in the programme. Session B for Registry 187 should explore: (1) what the comparative structure of 1 Sam 15:22 reveals about the hierarchy of inner-being orientations toward God — obedience and attentive listening as the higher register; (2) how this valuation relates to the strength/capacity cluster's broader understanding of inner-being agency before God; (3) the relationship between sha.ma and qashav as paired inner-being acts that together constitute the responsive orientation of the person toward God. |

| DIM-068-SD015 | MEDIUM |  | Goodness (Reg 67, C10) cooccurrence: 9 verses. Goodness is paired with grace in the OT divine character formula (Exo 34:6: 'abounding in steadfast love and faithfulness'; Psa 25:7: 'according to your steadfast love remember me, for the sake of your goodness'). Session D should examine whether grace and goodness name adjacent or overlapping divine character attributes, whether the inner-being experience of goodness received is distinguishable from grace received, and how the goodness of God functions as a category distinct from grace in the structure of divine inner-being disposition toward the creature. |

The cross-cluster root family connection makes this one of the most structurally significant lexical bridges in the programme. |

| SP-067-026 | MEDIUM |  | [Migrated from catalogue GAP-S5-001 2026-04-28 — programme-level methodology question, not a per-word analytical question] Where a registry's word functions in more than one dimension (both as a character attribute and as a comparative/evaluative operator), does the co-occurrence and correlation data reveal different connection patterns for different modes of the word? |
| SP-067-027 | MEDIUM |  | [v1.8 obslog SD pointer] R187 (strength) shows 32 shared verses with R67 (goodness) — the highest co-occurrence in current data. Is this co-occurrence conceptual (strength and goodness structurally related) or contextual/formulaic (co-appearance in wisdom/Psalms contexts without semantic connection)? Target: R187 (strength) Connecting term: H2896A — co-occurrence in wisdom and Psalms literature Evidence basis: Q&A-144, data package §5 (current co-occurrence counts) |
| DIM-064-SD007 | MEDIUM | G0863H | The Synoptic forgiveness pronouncements (Luk 5:20-24; Mar 2:5-10; Mat 9:2-6) generate a Christological argument: Jesus claims authority to forgive sins that is challenged as belonging exclusively to God ('who can forgive sins but God alone?' Mar 2:7). Jesus' response is to demonstrate the authority through a healing. The authority to forgive is here presented as equivalent to divine authority. Session D question: does the authority corpus (197, C20) address the relationship between divine authority and the capacity to forgive at scale? Is forgiveness structurally dependent on authority in a way that the C20 analysis should examine? Confirmed by cooccurrence (10 shared verses) and dimension overlap (5). Cannot be resolved within registry 064 alone. |
| DIM-6-SD001 | MEDIUM |  | C16 contains a substantial concentration of GOD-dominant Agency/Power and Transformation groups across anointing, consecration, and blessing — God appointing, transforming, restoring, forgiving, and blessing as inner-being acts directed toward the human person. Session D should synthesise how God's inner-being activity toward persons is distributed across the full programme and whether C16 represents a distinct domain of divine inner-being engagement (endowment/consecration) distinguishable from other clusters. |
| DIM-068-SD016 | LOW |  | Anointing (Reg 6, C16) and blessing (Reg 194, C16) cooccurrence: 7 verses each. Grace co-occurs with both C16 registries at the same level, suggesting a possible C16-C17 connection zone. Anointing, blessing, and grace may constitute a semantic network of divine bestowal — each naming a distinct modality through which the divine disposition of favour is concretely extended to the creature. Session D should examine whether grace, anointing, and blessing share a common inner-being ground (the creature as recipient of divine favour) and what distinguishes them: grace as relational disposition freely given; blessing as the concrete content of what is given; anointing as the specific act of set-apart conferral. |
| DIM-42-SD001 | HIGH |  | The Theological/Divine-Human cluster within delight/joy registries — God's pleasure/displeasure as the criterion of acceptance — names a structural feature of the inner-being relationship with God: divine inner states (delight, pleasure, exultation) are constitutive of the human inner-being condition. Session D should examine divine inner states as a programme-level category and their relationship to human inner states across all clusters. |
| DIM-42-SD002 | MEDIUM |  | Morally negative joy/gladness appears across multiple C03 registries: 355-002 and 365-002 (R97), 1096-001 (R132), 634-002 (R186). The same inner-being faculty appears in both ordered and disordered forms. Session D should examine how the positive/negative pairing of inner states recurs across the programme — whether positive inner capacities have systematic corrupted counterparts. |
| DIM-42-SD003 | HIGH |  | Two groups name the inner life as designed toward an end: 378-002 (chara — joy as the goal/completion of the inner life in God) and 6701-001 (miph.la.ah — wondrous works designed to evoke wonder). Researcher note: this may also include what God intends as the inner state of the person. These groups point toward a dimension the existing vocabulary does not name — the inner life as oriented toward a divinely intended goal. Session D should examine whether a new vocabulary category is needed. |
| DIM-42-SD004 | MEDIUM |  | Group 3090-001 (hēdonē) occupies a counter-position to the positive delight vocabulary. The pleasure/delight tension in the NT — where hēdonē names the adversary of the ordered inner life — is a significant cross-registry pattern. Session D should examine the structured opposition between competing inner orientations as a programme-level finding. |
| DIM-187-SD001 | HIGH |  | Programme-level registry validation gap identified during C20 Dimension Review (2026-04-08). Root cause: STEP Bible treats suffix variants of a root (e.g. H8085G vs H8085H for sha.ma) as sub-glosses rather than as independently linked related terms. The Session A extraction pipeline follows STEP's relational architecture and consequently does not surface sub-gloss variants as candidates for independent extraction. H8085H (sha.ma — to hear/obey) was not extracted; H7181 (qashav — to attend/listen) was extracted but STEP shows its related-word set as identical to H8085G, masking the sha.ma/qashav relationship. These are foundational inner-being terms — the hearing/obeying and attending/listening cluster is central to the biblical account of the inner person's orientation toward God. Session D must initiate a backward validation sweep: for every registered term, check whether its STEP sub-gloss variants were independently assessed for extraction eligibility. Priority: terms where the H/G/I suffix pattern produces distinct semantic content. This sweep should be scoped and executed before Session D synthesis conclusions are drawn on the hearing/obeying dimension. |
| DIM-187-SD002 | MEDIUM |  | The oikos/oikia family in Reg 187 generates groups across at least seven distinct inner-being dimensions (Moral Character, Transformation, Agency/Power, Relational Disposition, Volition, Divine-Human Correspondence, Vitality/Existence). The household vocabulary functions as a structural metaphor for the inner life rather than naming a single characteristic. Session D should assess whether the household/dwelling family constitutes a distinct metaphorical register in the biblical account of the inner person. |
| DIM-187-SD003 | MEDIUM |  | The AMTS root family (am.mits, am.tsah, o.mets in Reg 187) is closely cognate with the CHAZAQ family (Reg 33/C08) in the biblical courage formula chazaq ve-amats (be strong and courageous). The split of these two roots across C08 (courage, verb forms) and C20 (strength, adjective/noun forms) is analytically significant. Session D should synthesise the full courage formula by reading the C08 and C20 groups together, addressing the verb/adjective split within the same root complex. |
| DIM-187-SD004 | MEDIUM |  | The directionally-determined pattern appears with particular density in C20 strength vocabulary. The same Hebrew root generates groups in opposite registers — divine greatness and human pride (GADAL), strength as divine gift and strength as misplaced trust (OZ, KO.ACH, CHAYIL), sure-footedness and collapse (TSAV). This pattern, established as a structural principle across earlier clusters, is most concentrated in C20. Session D should assess whether the strength/power cluster adds a specific contribution to the directionally-determined principle beyond what C08 (virtue/vice) and C09 (pride/majesty) already established. |
| DIM-068-SD003 | MEDIUM |  | C20 power cluster dimension overlap: strength (187), power (196), authority (197), might (198), dominion (199) — all share all 3 grace dimensions (Relational Disposition 9 groups, Moral Character 1, Dependence/Creatureliness 1). 2Cor 12:9 makes the structural connection explicit: power made perfect in weakness, grace as its modality. Session D should examine whether grace consistently names the relational mode through which divine power reaches the creature precisely where human power fails, and whether Dependence/Creatureliness is the inner condition that opens the person to both grace and power. |
| DIM-111-SD001 | HIGH |  | Strength/power/authority/dominion registries (Reg 187/196/197/198/199) share all 4 of mercy dimensions. Structural question: is mercy the moral direction of power toward the vulnerable? Session D to examine whether power and mercy form a structural inner-being pair. |
| DIM-103-SD026 | MEDIUM | G0025 | C20 cluster (strength/might/authority/dominion — Regs 187/198/197/199) shares all 8 dimensions with love and co-occurs in 69-105 verses. This may be a registry-breadth effect, but the co-occurrence is genuine and large. Question: does the biblical vocabulary connect love and power structurally? Is love the form that power takes in the kingdom of God, and power the form that love takes in creation? The 1Cor 13 sequence — love as greater than gifts of power and knowledge — suggests love and power are in deliberate tension in the NT. |
| DIM-064-SD019 | MEDIUM | G0859 | 19 shared verses between forgiveness (064) and strength (187) is the second-highest cooccurrence signal in this registry's corpus. The forgiveness passages frequently invoke divine power, exaltation, and authority in the same context as forgiveness: Acts 5:31 ('God exalted him at his right hand as Leader and Savior to give repentance and forgiveness of sins'), Num 14:19 ('according to the greatness of your steadfast love'). Session D question: does the programme's strength/power cluster (C20) address the relationship between divine omnipotence and the capacity to forgive at the scale Scripture describes? Is there an inner-being statement about divine power embedded in the forgiveness corpus that the C20 synthesis should address? Confirmed by 19 shared verses (strongest unexpected cooccurrence signal in this registry). |
